Prevention, through regular checks suited to the needs of the elderly, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>plays a decisive role, as does rehabilitation, by maintaining the functional capacities of \u000a</p>\u000a<p>elderly persons, with a resulting decrease in the cost of investments in health care and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>social services.5 \u000a</p>\u000a<p>  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> 2 General comment No.14, para. 11.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> 3 General comment No. 6, paras. 34-35.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> 4 General comment No.14, para. 25.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> \u000a5 General comment No. 6, para. 35.  </p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p>37. The Committee also noted that national policies should help older people to continue \u000a</p>\u000a<p>to live in their own homes as long as possible, through the restoration, development and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>improvement of homes and their adaptation to the ability of those persons to gain access to \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and use them.6  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>38. In addition, the Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities includes the \u000a</p>\u000a<p>concept of &#8220;universal design&#8221;, which may be of benefit to older persons. It is defined, in \u000aarticle 2, as the design of products, environments, programmes and services to be usable by \u000a</p>\u000a<p>all people, to the greatest extent possible, without the need for adaptation or specialized \u000a</p>\u000a<p>design and develops State obligations regarding the promotion of the availability and use of \u000a</p>\u000a<p>universally designed goods, services, equipment and facilities. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>39. The Madrid International Plan of Action on Ageing recommends the establishment \u000a</p>\u000a<p>of preventive and curative care, including rehabilitation and sexual health care, recognizing \u000a</p>\u000a<p>that health-promotion and disease-prevention activities throughout life need to focus on \u000a</p>\u000a<p>maintaining independence, on prevention, and on avoidance of delay in disease treatment, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>as well as on improving the health and quality of life of older persons who already have a \u000a</p>\u000a<p>disability.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>40. Particular actions are encouraged to provide counsel and guide persons on healthy \u000a</p>\u000a<p>lifestyles and self-care.7 Detailed actions are designed to further encourage the adoption of \u000a</p>\u000a<p>care measures that take into account age and the diversity of older persons, including \u000a</p>\u000a<p>primary care, acute care, rehabilitation, long-term and palliative care, self-care and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>gerontological services, provided both by formal and informal caregivers. Special attention \u000a</p>\u000a<p>is given to the importance of developing housing options for older persons that encourage \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and reduce barriers to independence, including in public spaces, transportation and other \u000a</p>\u000a<p>services.8 \u000a</p>\u000a<p>41. At the regional level, the African Charter on Human and Peoples&#8217; Rights recognizes \u000athe right to health and the role played by the family in society, and the right of older \u000a</p>\u000a<p>persons to special measures of protection in keeping with their physical or moral needs \u000a</p>\u000a<p>(arts. 16 and 18). The draft protocol to the Charter on the rights of older persons in Africa \u000a</p>\u000a<p>makes clear mention of care and support and access to health services, including in conflict \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and disaster situations. For instance, according to article 12, States should identify, promote \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and strengthen traditional support systems, including medical home based care, to enhance \u000a</p>\u000a<p>the ability of families and communities to care for older family members and adopt policies \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and legislation that provide incentives to all stakeholders including adult children, to \u000a</p>\u000a<p>support older persons in their communities, ensuring that they remain at home for as long \u000a</p>\u000a<p>as possible. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>42. In the Americas, the Additional Protocol to the American Convention on Human \u000a</p>\u000a<p>Rights in the Area of Economic, Social and Cultural Rights recognizes the right to social \u000a</p>\u000a<p>security and the right to health (arts. 9 and 10). Moreover, the Inter-American Convention \u000a</p>\u000a<p>on Protecting the Human Rights of Older Persons dedicates an article to the right of older \u000a</p>\u000a<p>persons to receive long-term care, while maintaining their independence and autonomy. A \u000a</p>\u000a<p>comprehensive approach to the right to health has been adopted in article 19, by including \u000a</p>\u000a<p>health promotion, prevention and care of disease at all stages, including rehabilitation and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>palliative care.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>43. In Europe, the Council of Europe recommendation on the promotion of human \u000a</p>\u000a<p>rights of older persons dedicates a specific title to care, in which it recommends that States \u000a</p>\u000a<p>promote a multidimensional approach to health and social care for older persons and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>encourage cooperation among the competent services. It mentions several measures, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> 6 Ibid., para. 33. \u000a</p>\u000a<p> 7 Madrid International Plan of Action on Ageing, paras. 67 (d) and 105.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> 8 Madrid International Plan of Action on Ageing, paras. 90 (f) and 98-100. </p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p>including training, consent to medical care and regulation of care delivery, in home, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>residential, institutional, long-term and palliative care. \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> B. Autonomy \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b> 1. Definition and scope \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>44. Autonomy refers to the principle or right of individuals or groups of individuals to \u000a</p>\u000a<p>determine their own rules and preferences. It includes the freedom and capacity to make \u000a</p>\u000a<p>one&#8217;s own decisions and the legal capacity to exercise those decisions. Autonomy \u000aencompasses three main elements: an individual aspect, which includes the capacity to \u000a</p>\u000a<p>make decisions; an economic and financial aspect, understood as self-sufficiency and the \u000a</p>\u000a<p>ability to generate and receive income; and a societal aspect, which means the existence of \u000a</p>\u000a<p>communities and environments that are age-sensitive and age-friendly in order to ensure \u000a</p>\u000a<p>that older persons are able to decide or act for themselves.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>45. The full enjoyment of autonomy has broad scope, including not only the right to \u000a</p>\u000a<p>equal recognition before the law, legal capacity, dignity, self-determination, empowerment \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and decision-making, but also the right to choose where to live, the right to work, the right \u000a</p>\u000a<p>to vote and to the right to participate actively in all spheres of society. The denial or \u000a</p>\u000a<p>restriction of legal capacity directly impacts the autonomy of older persons, as they will no \u000a</p>\u000a<p>longer be able to exercise these other rights, including making decisions regarding civil, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>commercial, administrative, judicial or health-related matters concerning their well-being.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>46. Autonomy and independence are mutually reinforcing and are often used \u000a</p>\u000a<p>interchangeably in legal instruments and frameworks. While autonomy refers to the ability \u000a</p>\u000a<p>to exercise freedom of choice and control over decisions affecting one&#8217;s life, including with \u000athe help of someone if needed, independence means to live in the community without \u000a</p>\u000a<p>assistance or, at least, where the amount of help does not subject older persons to the \u000a</p>\u000a<p>decisions of others. In that sense, the concept of independence is broader than autonomy, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>while autonomy may better reflect the reality of older persons, considering that with age, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>the need for assistance tends to increase. \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> 2. Legal capacity and equal recognition before the law \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>47. Various tools exist to measure the degree of physical, cognitive and psychological \u000a</p>\u000a<p>autonomy, which take into account: mobility, communication and tasks performed on a \u000a</p>\u000a<p>daily basis to determine the degree of functional and mental autonomy of an older person. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>In terms of a human rights-based approach, however, the individual aspect of autonomy \u000a</p>\u000a<p>refers not only to functional autonomy but also mainly to the right to equal recognition \u000a</p>\u000a<p>before the law and legal capacity. This implies being recognized as a legal person before \u000a</p>\u000a<p>the law and having the ability to exercise rights as a legal person. Consequently, the will \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and preferences of older persons are respected, which allows for the exercise of free and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>informed consent. Older persons should therefore be consulted about and involved in any \u000a</p>\u000a<p>decision affecting their well-being. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>48. Legal capacity is a key aspect of autonomy, allowing older persons to exercise civil, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>political, economic, social and cultural rights. In circumstances where an older person is \u000a</p>\u000a<p>partially or completely unable to look after their own interests because of a mental \u000a</p>\u000a<p>condition, such as dementia, or an extreme state of physical frailty, there may be a need for \u000a</p>\u000a<p>supported decision making. There is a need to ensure, however, that in such instances a \u000a</p>\u000a<p>person is not stripped of his or her legal capacity by guardianship measures, which remove \u000a</p>\u000a<p>their ability to make decisions about certain aspects of their lives. It is important to note in \u000a</p>\u000a<p>this regard that the Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities provides that a \u000a</p>\u000a<p>person&#8217;s status as a person with a disability or the existence of impairment must never be a \u000aground for denying legal capacity. In its general comment No. 1 on article 12 of the \u000a</p>\u000a<p>Convention, the Committee on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities highlighted that the </p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p>Convention does not legitimatize the denial of legal capacity based on perceived or actual \u000a</p>\u000a<p>deficits in mental capacity.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>49. The Independent Expert noted the positive trend in recent years to reform legal \u000a</p>\u000a<p>capacity and guardianship laws. General comment No.1 also provides guidance to States \u000a</p>\u000a<p>parties on reforming their current laws and legislation&#8212;in particular mental-health \u000alegislation that deprives persons with disabilities of their legal capacity&#8212;and encourages \u000aStates to replace regimes that provide for substitute decision-making by supported decision-\u000a</p>\u000a<p>making.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>50. Effective safeguards for ensuring the autonomy of older persons should be \u000a</p>\u000a<p>developed and implemented to ensure the respect of the rights, wishes and preferences of \u000a</p>\u000a<p>older persons and to avoid undue interference. The Committee on the Rights of Persons \u000a</p>\u000a<p>with Disabilities provides guidance on supportive measures, stressing that if, after \u000a</p>\u000a<p>significant efforts have been made, it is still not practicable to determine the will and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>preferences of an individual, the &#8220;best interpretation of will and preferences&#8221; must replace \u000athe &#8220;best interests&#8221; determinations.9 In addition, articles 11 and 30 of the Inter-American \u000aConvention on Protecting the Human Rights of Older Persons could also guide States on \u000a</p>\u000a<p>this issue.   \u000a</p>\u000a<p>51. Legal capacity has particular relevance for older persons regarding making \u000a</p>\u000a<p>fundamental decisions regarding their social and health care, in particular medical \u000a</p>\u000a<p>treatment. The respect for and the strengthening of older persons&#8217; autonomy in care settings \u000ameans that they must be able to give consent to, refuse or choose an alternative medical \u000a</p>\u000a<p>intervention.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>52. In his report on the fundamental role played by informed consent in respecting, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>protecting and fulfilling the right to health, the Special Rapporteur on the right of everyone \u000a</p>\u000a<p>to the enjoyment of the highest attainable standard of physical and mental health \u000a</p>\u000a<p>highlighted that informed consent is not a mere acceptance of a medical intervention, but a \u000a</p>\u000a<p>voluntary and sufficiently informed decision, safeguarding the right of the patient to be \u000a</p>\u000a<p>involved in medical decision-making, and assigning associated duties and obligations to \u000a</p>\u000a<p>health-care providers.10 Its ethical and legal normative justifications stem from its \u000a</p>\u000a<p>promotion of patient autonomy, self-determination, bodily integrity and well-being.11 Older \u000a</p>\u000a<p>persons should therefore be encouraged to plan in advance any decisions regarding their \u000a</p>\u000a<p>care. Advance planning allows them to prevent abuses that often occur in older age. \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> 3. Adequate standard of living and social protection \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>53. Living with autonomy is closely linked to the right to an adequate standard of living, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>social security, income security and an adequate public pension. The important role of \u000a</p>\u000a<p>social protection is recognized in several international and regional instruments and should \u000a</p>\u000a<p>be fully implemented and enforced in order to promote the autonomy of older persons. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>54. Poverty contributes to the decline of older persons&#8217; autonomy and is an impediment \u000ato accessing adequate nutrition, water and sanitation, and social and health care. Not \u000a</p>\u000a<p>receiving a pension or not having insurance often inhibits older persons from seeking \u000a</p>\u000a<p>medical attention, putting them at a higher risk of social isolation and of being denied \u000a</p>\u000a<p>access to health care. Even for those who have insurance, there are delays in seeking public \u000a</p>\u000a<p>health care or obtaining prescription drugs because of the financial burden. Financial \u000a</p>\u000a<p>services, such as loans or mortgages, or insurance are often not available to older persons or \u000a</p>\u000a<p>are prohibitively expensive because of the inappropriate use of age as a criterion, including \u000a</p>\u000a<p>for determining risk.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> 9 General comment No. 1, para. 21. \u000a</p>\u000a<p> 10 See A/64/272. \u000a</p>\u000a<p> 11 Ibid., para. 9. </p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p>55. Social transfers and pension schemes often account for the largest part of the \u000a</p>\u000a<p>resources of older persons and significantly reduce their at-risk-of poverty rate by ensuring \u000a</p>\u000a<p>some financial security.12 They are thus an important factor in enabling older persons to live \u000a</p>\u000a<p>autonomously and not be dependent on intra-family transfers or any other private income. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>Access to social insurance schemes and pensions, particularly for women, rural workers, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and persons who worked in the informal sector, is essential for persons to live an \u000a</p>\u000a<p>autonomous life when they become old.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>56. The Independent Expert strongly encourages the review of austerity measures and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>fiscal consolidation programmes, especially those which may negatively impact the \u000a</p>\u000a<p>economic and financial autonomy of older persons by increasing the risk of poverty, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>exclusion and insecurity in old age. Social protection should be strengthened, both \u000a</p>\u000a<p>contributory and non-contributory schemes, by increasing resources, improving the \u000a</p>\u000a<p>availability of and access to services, through communication and by removing any \u000a</p>\u000a<p>obstacles to receiving the benefits. \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> 4. Right to work  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>57. It is also important to ensure the right to work by fostering the active economic \u000a</p>\u000a<p>participation of older persons as long as they are willing or able. Older persons have much \u000a</p>\u000a<p>to contribute, based on the considerable skills, wisdom, expertise and experience they have \u000a</p>\u000a<p>acquired throughout their professional life, and they should be encouraged to continue \u000a</p>\u000a<p>working if they wish. Therefore, workplaces and job options that include older workers \u000a</p>\u000a<p>should be developed, as well as voluntary work. Older persons have indispensable roles in \u000a</p>\u000a<p>assisting peers and participating in intergenerational activities by helping younger \u000a</p>\u000a<p>generations, and by contributing to the education of grandchildren. This will help reinforce \u000a</p>\u000a<p>social cohesion and the interaction of older persons with other members of the community.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> 5. Right to adequate housing and accessibility  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>58. Age-sensitive communities and age-friendly environments are preconditions for \u000a</p>\u000a<p>older persons to live with autonomy and remain integrated in society. Older persons should \u000a</p>\u000a<p>be able to live in environments that are safe and adaptable to their personal preferences and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>changing capacities. The environment should therefore be adapted to their needs. This \u000a</p>\u000a<p>should include, for instance, outdoor and public spaces, the physical environment, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>buildings, transportation, social participation and inclusion, civic participation and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>employment, information and communications and other facilities and services in both \u000a</p>\u000a<p>urban and rural areas. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>59. Physical barriers significantly undermine the autonomy of older persons and deny \u000a</p>\u000a<p>them access to basic services, including health care. When older persons are unable to \u000a</p>\u000a<p>drive, the lack of public transport, especially in rural and remote areas, presents serious \u000a</p>\u000a<p>challenges. Even if they are able to access public transport, older persons still require a \u000a</p>\u000a<p>reasonable level of physical fitness, the competence to follow bus-route directions, the \u000a</p>\u000a<p>ability to endure long rides and bus transfers, and a safe environment to wait and to walk in. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>60. Accessibility encompasses access to infrastructure, buildings, transportation, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>information and care settings in urban and rural areas. States should provide comprehensive \u000a</p>\u000a<p>barrier-free accessibility environments and work towards eliminating existing barriers. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>Public buildings, facilities, roads and transportation should be easily accessible. For this to \u000a</p>\u000a<p>occur, revisions to building control acts and urban planning codes, with the inclusion of \u000a</p>\u000a<p>standards of universal design in a variety of sectors and facilities, may be needed. States \u000a</p>\u000a<p>should also provide training to all stakeholders involved in adapting public spaces to \u000a</p>\u000a<p>  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> 12 A/HRC/14/31. </p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p>demographic changes: including engineers, designers, architects, urban planners, transport \u000a</p>\u000a<p>authorities and conductors, service providers and members of the academic community.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>61. Another important element to leading an autonomous life is housing. Older persons \u000a</p>\u000a<p>have the right to choose where to live, and, to the extent possible, housing should be \u000a</p>\u000a<p>adapted to their needs. Should they wish If they feel the need to change or adapt their \u000a</p>\u000a<p>homes because of their mobility requirements, States should secure rent- free \u000a</p>\u000a<p>accommodation, or at least provide rent subsidies or , credit facilities , to help older persons \u000a</p>\u000a<p>afford to remain in their own houses.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>62. Housing options for older persons, including co-housing based on intergenerational \u000a</p>\u000a<p>communities, age-adapted homes and flat-sharing concepts should be further developed. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>Other schemes that include bank credit facilitation, tax incentives and subsidies, involving \u000a</p>\u000a<p>both private and public sectors should be encouraged so as to build accessible and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>appropriate housing for older persons. Particular attention should be given to older women \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and widows, who tend to live alone with no family support.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> 6. Participation and social inclusion \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>63. An age-friendly community is one that also considers an older person&#8217;s political \u000ainvolvement, meaning that age cannot be a justification for any exclusion from decision-\u000a</p>\u000a<p>making processes and active citizenship. The right to participate in political and public life \u000a</p>\u000a<p>includes the possibility of being able to vote, and of being elected, sometimes requiring the \u000a</p>\u000a<p>availability of facilities and materials that are accessible to older persons, including in \u000a</p>\u000a<p>nursing and institutional care settings. States should provide access to transport to and from \u000a</p>\u000a<p>polling stations, as well as to attend meetings of public authorities and to participate in \u000a</p>\u000a<p>trade-union and advocacy efforts.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>64. The involvement of older persons in the electoral system has been declining, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>especially in the age group of 80 and above, as a result of discriminatory practices leading \u000a</p>\u000a<p>to social exclusion and political marginalization. The participation of older persons should \u000a</p>\u000a<p>therefore be institutionalized through forums, or consultative, coordinating or advisory \u000a</p>\u000a<p>bodies at the local, regional and national level.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> 7. Education, training and lifelong learning \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>65. Autonomy includes access to education, training and lifelong learning that will help \u000a</p>\u000a<p>maintain self-esteem and extend knowledge and adaptability to cultural and social changes, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>especially regarding advances in information technology. The target of educational \u000a</p>\u000a<p>programmes should not be limited to older persons only, in order to promote inter-\u000a</p>\u000a<p>generational learning and understanding. States should develop specific pedagogical \u000a</p>\u000a<p>methods for teaching older persons, such as master&#8217;s degree programmes in geronto-\u000apedagogics. Particular attention should be given to information technology and computing \u000a</p>\u000a<p>training programmes.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>66. With the rapid evolution of new technologies, autonomy also means having access \u000a</p>\u000a<p>to the Internet and being able to use information and communications technology and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>services. When online registration is sometimes the only or easiest way to purchase goods \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and services, older persons should be able to access these goods and services without \u000a</p>\u000a<p>depending on others. Older persons constitute a distinct consumer group, with specific \u000a</p>\u000a<p>needs, interests and preferences. Technologies can contribute to strengthening an older \u000a</p>\u000a<p>person&#8217;s autonomy by adapting products to meet the changes incurred with age, such as \u000athrough adjusting the size of objects or through offering technical support.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>67. Autonomy also means being able to access cultural activities through the availability \u000a</p>\u000a<p>of transport, subsidized tickets for cultural activities and reduced fees or free entrance for \u000a</p>\u000a<p>older persons. Services such as closed-captioning and open subtitled for those with hearing \u000a</p>\u000a<p>impairments should be provided in theatres, museums and other cultural-related facilities.  </p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p><b> 8. Right to health \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>68. In care settings, autonomy means that older persons are able to participate in the \u000a</p>\u000a<p>formulation, monitoring and evaluation of their care services, as well as in clinical trials in \u000a</p>\u000a<p>order to determine the effect of medication.13 Older persons should no longer be seen as \u000a</p>\u000a<p>passive recipients of care but as active users.14 This change of paradigm from passive to \u000a</p>\u000a<p>active care is a shift from care provided for people to care provided with people.15  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>69. Older persons should receive health education, and information on the social and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>health-care services available to them, on their rights in care settings. They should also \u000a</p>\u000a<p>receive guidance on adequate nutrition and on different pathologies, including \u000a</p>\u000a<p>communicable and non-communicable diseases. In this context, self-care can help older \u000a</p>\u000a<p>persons to participate actively in and manage their own health. \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> C. Care \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b> 1. Definition and scope \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>70. There is no universally accepted definition of care.16 Care aims at maintaining or \u000a</p>\u000a<p>regaining the optimum level of physical, mental and emotional wellbeing and to prevent or \u000a</p>\u000a<p>delay the onset of disease. While a distinction is generally made between social and health \u000a</p>\u000a<p>care, depending on where and by whom such services are provided, care should be \u000a</p>\u000a<p>understood in a complementary and integrated manner for the benefit of older persons. It \u000a</p>\u000a<p>comprises a range of services, facilities, knowledge and support aimed at increasing the \u000a</p>\u000a<p>individual&#8217;s physical, mental and social well-being, provided either by formal or informal \u000acarers, and through the public or the private sector. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>71. Care encompasses services such as assistance with the activities of daily life, social \u000a</p>\u000a<p>income, protection and security, as well as health promotion and disease prevention, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>treatment and rehabilitation and the provision of primary, secondary and tertiary health \u000a</p>\u000a<p>care, in ambulatory, institutional or home settings. Equality of access to social and health \u000a</p>\u000a<p>care throughout the life of older persons is also the basis of an autonomous life and active \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and healthy ageing. \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> 2. Care settings \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>72. Home and family care is the most common form of care for older persons in many \u000a</p>\u000a<p>countries, where care of older persons is regarded very much seen as the responsibility of \u000a</p>\u000a<p>the family. While for many older persons, family care is the preferred option in old age, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>there is a need for adequate parallel support to family members and other informal \u000a</p>\u000a<p>caregivers. This would include respite care services, needs assessment, counselling and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>advice, self-support groups and practical training in care-giving, as well as information \u000a</p>\u000a<p>about measures to protect carers&#8217; own physical and mental health, weekend breaks, and \u000aintegrated planning of care for older persons and families. Including family carers into the \u000a</p>\u000a<p>social security system is particularly important given that caregivers are often women who \u000a</p>\u000a<p>are not gainfully employed and in view of the increasing need for care in ageing societies. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> \u000a13\u000a</p>\u000a<p> See A/HRC/18/37, para. 16.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> \u000a14\u000a</p>\u000a<p> Economic Commission for Europe policy brief on ageing No. 15, &#8220;Innovative and empowering \u000a</p>\u000a<p>strategies for care&#8221; (2015), p. 5. \u000a</p>\u000a<p> \u000a15\u000a</p>\u000a<p> Ibid.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> 16 World Health Organization glossary of terms for community health care and services for older \u000a</p>\u000a<p>persons (2004). The glossary (www.who.int/kobe_centre/ageing/ahp_vol5_glossary.pdf) could \u000a</p>\u000a<p>provide guidance on the scope of &#8220;social care&#8221;, &#8220;health care&#8221;, &#8220;acute health care&#8221;, &#8220;ambulatory care&#8221;, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>&#8220;long-term care&#8221;, &#8220;home care or domiciliary care&#8221;, &#8220;adult care homes&#8221;, &#8220;day care centres&#8221;, &#8220;hospice \u000a</p>\u000a<p>care&#8221;, &#8220;palliative care&#8221;, &#8220;residential care&#8221; and &#8220;formal and informal caregivers&#8221;.  </p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p>73. The Independent Expert emphasizes that the assistance provided by family members \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and communities cannot substitute for States&#8217; obligations to promote and protect all human \u000arights of older persons. She also notes the radical changes some societies are undergoing, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>such as changes in family patterns or lifestyles, as well as challenges related to migration \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and urbanization, which need to be taken into account. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>74. The institutionalization of care, while it can be the result of an autonomous decision \u000a</p>\u000a<p>of a person as he or she becomes older, can often take the form of forced institutionalization \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and compulsory placements, especially when no other forms of care are available for the \u000a</p>\u000a<p>individual or when relatives are unable or unwilling to provide care.17 When proper legal \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and institutional mechanisms and procedures are in place in care settings, thus ensuring \u000a</p>\u000a<p>freedom of choice and informed consent, older persons in need of care can lead a life with \u000a</p>\u000a<p>dignity. It is therefore crucial to ensure older persons&#8217; autonomy, in particular when it \u000acomes to any decision-making affecting their care. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>75. Flexible and open care institutions have been established in several countries in \u000a</p>\u000a<p>order to avoid such institutionalization, with free provision of medical home care, including \u000a</p>\u000a<p>administration of medication and infusions. Such services have improved the quality of life \u000a</p>\u000a<p>of older persons by enabling them to stay at home. \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> 3. Quality control and accessibility \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>76. Ensuring quality control in care settings is one of the main challenges. It has been \u000a</p>\u000a<p>pointed out that the office hours of social and health-care services were often anti-social \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and to the disadvantage of older persons. For example, older persons are often required to \u000a</p>\u000a<p>arrive early in the morning and queue to receive a number that will correspond to their \u000a</p>\u000a<p>attendance. Such an approach results in long waits in uncomfortable and even inhumane \u000a</p>\u000a<p>conditions, with a lack of appropriate assistance that takes into account their specific needs. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>Associated with this, is the shortage of social and health-care professionals with formal \u000a</p>\u000a<p>education in geriatrics and gerontology, and with gender and disability-based expertise.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>77. Most health-care undergraduate programmes do not include geriatric and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>gerontology in their curricula, with the result that health-care professionals are not fully \u000a</p>\u000a<p>trained to help and understand older persons. For older persons who do not have family or \u000a</p>\u000a<p>community support, having to rely exclusively on formal caregivers without such training \u000a</p>\u000a<p>or qualifications can make their lives particularly difficult.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>78. The fragmentation of care also causes problems owing to the co-existence of many \u000a</p>\u000a<p>units that are not integrated into the network of services, and which results in hours spent in \u000a</p>\u000a<p>filling complicated forms to access both social and health-care services and facilities. It also \u000a</p>\u000a<p>results in poor quality service provision, inefficient use of available resources and low user \u000a</p>\u000a<p>satisfaction. This may often be due to the lack of coordination between the different levels \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and places of care, especially in hospitals. Consequently, older users experience a lack of \u000a</p>\u000a<p>continuity in care, together with a lack of consistency in the provision of services for \u000a</p>\u000a<p>meeting their needs. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>79. Equitable access to health services is another challenge faced by many countries. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>Care facilities, goods and related services need to be made available, accessible, affordable \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and acceptable, and be of good quality for all older persons. There is a need to ensure that \u000a</p>\u000a<p>these all older persons have access to the same services irrespective of the type of care and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>the place of residence, including in urban, rural and remote areas. In this connection, older \u000a</p>\u000a<p>persons may require access to transportation, information and communication on care-\u000a</p>\u000a<p>related programmes and facilities that meet their requirements and needs.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> 17 See E/2012/51, para. 25.  </p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p>80. Care work is often characterized by a heavy workload, long working hours and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>emotional exhaustion; and care workers tend to be undervalued, underpaid and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>undertrained. There is evidence that the satisfaction of persons receiving care correlates \u000a</p>\u000a<p>with the well-being of their care workers. \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> 4. Elder abuse and violence against older persons \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>81. Abuse of and violence against older persons in home and institutional care settings, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>by both formal and informal caregivers, is still a taboo subject in many countries and an \u000a</p>\u000a<p>underreported problem. These older persons may be subjected to intimidation, aggression, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>inappropriate behaviour-control methods, negligence, or a failure to provide the appropriate \u000a</p>\u000a<p>or necessary care, among other forms of ill-treatment.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>82. Risk factors for abuse and violence in care settings may relate to institutional \u000a</p>\u000a<p>aspects, such as poorly trained staff, also as a result of a lack of career prospects and high \u000a</p>\u000a<p>turnover, as well as a culture of tolerance of aggression towards patients.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>83. Regular inspection and monitoring of home visits by certified care workers is \u000a</p>\u000a<p>necessary to ensure adequate quality of care and the protection of older persons, including \u000a</p>\u000a<p>against physical and mental violence, and from degrading treatment and neglect. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>84. Certain countries have implemented technical services provided by public \u000a</p>\u000a<p>prosecutors to help older persons who are involuntarily hospitalized or involuntarily \u000a</p>\u000a<p>receiving medical treatment. Offices of an ombudsperson have also been created to support \u000a</p>\u000a<p>older persons in expressing their needs and decisions, and to seek remedy.18 In care \u000a</p>\u000a<p>facilities, committees composed of older persons have been put in place to supervise and \u000a</p>\u000a<p>evaluate the quality of the services, especially regarding food, hygiene and interaction with \u000a</p>\u000a<p>staff. \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> 5. Geriatric services and palliative care \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>85. Older persons have different patterns of disease presentation than younger adults, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>they respond to treatments and therapies in different ways, and they frequently have \u000a</p>\u000a<p>complex social needs that are related to their chronic medical conditions. It is therefore \u000a</p>\u000a<p>important to ensure that sufficient doctors, staff and others qualified in geriatric health are \u000a</p>\u000a<p>available to ensure that these older persons can fully enjoy their right to health. \u000a</p>\u000a<p>86. Particular attention needs to be given to palliative care. In certain countries, this is \u000a</p>\u000a<p>not officially recognized as a medical speciality and the medicine used in such care is \u000a</p>\u000a<p>limited, for several reasons, including restrictive drug regulations, failure to implement a \u000a</p>\u000a<p>properly functioning supply and distribution system, and inadequate health-care system \u000a</p>\u000a<p>capacity.  \u000a</p>\u000a<p>87. The Independent Expert stresses that the Special Rapporteur on the right to health \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and the Special Rapporteur on torture and other cruel, inhuman or degrading treatment or \u000a</p>\u000a<p>punishment qualified the failure to ensure access to controlled medicines for the relief of \u000a</p>\u000a<p>pain and suffering as a threat to the fundamental right to health and the right to be free from \u000a</p>\u000a<p>cruel, inhuman and degrading treatment.19 \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> 6. Groups in focus \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>88. Particular attention should be given to specific groups of older persons when \u000a</p>\u000a<p>designing and implementing care policies, goods and services, in particular older women, \u000a</p>\u000a<p>  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> 18 Economic Commission for Latin America and the Caribbean. <i>Autonom&#237;a y dignidad en la vejez: \u000a</i></p>\u000a<p><i>Teor&#237;a y pr&#225;ctica en pol&#237;ticas de derechos de las personas mayores</i>, pp. 82-83. Available from \u000a</p>\u000a<p>http://repositorio.cepal.org/bitstream/handle/11362/37523/S1421014_es.pdf?sequence=1  \u000a</p>\u000a<p> \u000a19 A/HRC/22/53. </p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p>older persons with disabilities, and persons with chronic illnesses, non-communicable \u000a</p>\u000a<p>diseases and dementia. Gender, disability and cultural sensitivities should be promoted in \u000a</p>\u000a<p>care settings to allow older persons from different backgrounds to be informed effectively \u000a</p>\u000a<p>and to be able to make decisions. Specialized integrated social and health-care services \u000a</p>\u000a<p>should be developed and implemented to address dementia and other degenerative diseases \u000a</p>\u000a<p>that lead to dependency. \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> IV. Conclusions and recommendations \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b> A. Conclusions \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>89. <b>In the light of the intensity of ageing in many societies, there is a need to ensure \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>that older persons are enabled to lead autonomous lives. This also calls for a paradigm \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>shift that focuses on the inclusion of older persons in society at all levels, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>encompassing age-friendly communities and environments, as well as people-centred \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>models of care, and that promotes the autonomy and dignity of older persons. </b> \u000a</p>\u000a<p>90. <b>The design, implementation, monitoring and evaluation of any social and \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>health-care legislation, policies, programmes, strategies and settings must take into \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>account the respect for and the strengthening of older persons</b>&#8217; autonomy<b>. To promote \u000athis autonomy, effective care for older persons needs to integrate economic, physical, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>mental, social, spiritual and environmental factors. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>91. <b>The increasing prevalence of chronic and degenerative diseases in older age \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>poses challenges to the development and implementation of appropriate models of \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>care for older persons. The disease-focused approach in care at different levels needs \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>to be replaced by more effective and rights-based models in order to address the \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>specific needs of the most heterogeneous of all age groups.</b> \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b> B. Recommendations \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>92. <b> With a view to assisting States in developing and implementing appropriate \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>and effective measures to ensure the autonomy of older persons, including in social \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>and health-care settings, the Independent Expert makes the recommendations set out \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>below.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>  Legal, institutional and policy framework \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>93. <b>States must fully comply with their international obligations relating to \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>autonomy and care. The Independent Expert strongly encourages States to ratify all \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>the core human rights treaties, including the Convention on the Rights of Persons with \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>Disabilities, as well as all relevant regional instruments </b>&#8212;<b>notably the recently adopted \u000aInter-American Convention on the Human Rights of Older Persons, which makes \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>explicit reference to autonomy and care. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>94. <b>States should design and implement effectively national policies and action \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>plans on ageing that include specific provisions on autonomy and care, in a \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>comprehensive and intersectoral manner.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>95. <b>States should establish national councils on ageing, with older persons among \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>its members, to design and develop policies, including care, that correspond to their \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>needs and respect their autonomy. Such councils should guarantee pluralism, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>represent the diversity of older persons and receive sufficient funding so that they can \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>function properly and effectively.  </b></p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p><b>  Study and statistics \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>96. <b>States should ensure, nationwide, systemic and regular collection of \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>disaggregated statistical data, and carry out studies to assess the situation and needs \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>of older persons and develop targeted policies for older persons. Data must be used \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>sensibly to avoid stigmatization and potential misuse. Particular care should be \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>exercised when collecting and analysing data in order to respect and enforce data \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>protection and privacy. Older persons, including the very old and those in \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>institutionalized care settings, should systematically be included in surveys and official \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>statistics, in order to ensure a better differentiation for age and to better reflect the \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>broad heterogeneity of older persons. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>97. <b>States should constantly conduct researches and studies and collect age- and \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>gender-disaggregated data on abuse and violence against older persons in and outside \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>care settings in order to assess the current situation and take appropriate measures to \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>tackle elder abuse. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>  Discrimination, abuse and violence \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>98. <b>There is a need for national anti-discrimination strategies addressing \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>discrimination in a coherent and multifaceted way. States should, through legislation, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>prohibit direct or indirect discrimination against older persons, including in the \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>financial and insurance services sectors, as well as in care settings.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>  Legal capacity and equal recognition before the law \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>99. <b>The Independent Expert emphasizes that supported decision-making for \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>persons with intellectual or psychosocial disabilities, including older persons, is \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>essential for the respect of the autonomy of older persons and their individual rights, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>in their own capacity to give and withdraw informed consent for medical treatment, to \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>access justice, to vote, to marry, to work and to choose their place of residence. Judges \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>should be guided by the objective of ensuring that older persons can lead a self-\u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>determined and autonomous life for as long as possible. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>100. <b>Older persons must be provided with guarantees to ensure that their \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>preferences, their will and their best interests are taken into consideration in all \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>matters relevant to their life, including treatment, residence or assets. Conflict of \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>interest and undue influence should be regulated, especially in reference to family \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>members and caregivers.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>101. <b>Safeguards to free and informed consent should be adopted through legislation, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>policies and administrative procedures in conformity with international and regional \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>standards. Particular attention should be given to illiterate older persons or persons \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>with less formal education.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>  Adequate standard of living and social protection \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>102. <b>States must recognize the human right to social security in domestic law. Non-\u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>contributory and contributory pension schemes must be guided by international \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>human rights standards for the right to social security.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>103. <b>States should put in place social-protection and poverty-reduction \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>programmes, particularly designed for older persons, including those with disabilities. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>Human rights principles and standards should be integrated throughout the design, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>implementation and evaluation of social pensions, thereby ensuring that older persons \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>fully enjoy their human rights.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>104. <b>The Independent Expert recalls that the right to social security includes both \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>contributory and non-contributory benefits and benefits both in cash and in kind, and \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>that benefits should be adequate in amount and duration and accessible to all without </b></p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p><b>discrimination. Non-contributory old-age benefits or other assistance should be \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>provided for those without resources on reaching old age, with special attention to  be \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>given to older persons working in the informal sectors, older women, older widows, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>and those living in rural and remote areas.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>105. <b>In the light of the significant contribution of social transfers and pension \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>schemes, austerity measures and fiscal consolidation programmes should be revised in \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>order to ensure the provision of basic income to older persons, together with adequate \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>social and health-care services and support. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>106. <b>A thorough evidence-based analysis of the current and future needs of various \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>forms of care or affordable, accessible and barrier-free housing is essential for \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>meeting the immediate needs and for planning and preparing for the future and for \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>developing appropriate measures to ensure an inclusive society for all ages. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>  Right to work  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>107. <b>States should introduce incentives for employers and employees to extend \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>people&#8217;s <b>working lives beyond the mandatory retirement age. Workplace \u000aenvironments and working conditions should be adapted to older workers by \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>introducing flexible working arrangements, including phased retirement. The \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>Independent Expert also points out the importance of continued education and access \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>to new technologies, as well as vocational rehabilitation. States should ensure that \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>social security and pension systems do not penalize older workers who choose to work \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>beyond normal retirement age. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>108. <b>The contribution of older persons should be recognized and encouraged, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>including, but not limited to, their role in the care of family members, in household \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>maintenance and in voluntary and associative activities.</b> \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b>  Right to adequate housing and accessibility \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>109. <b>States should adopt housing policies that take into account the special needs of \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>older persons to enable these persons to live autonomously. Ageing at home requires \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>innovations in the housing sector, including alternative forms of housing for older \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>persons, such as mixed and designated communities and age-adapted homes, or flat-\u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>sharing concepts that promote intergenerational interaction. Alternative forms of \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>housing and the possibility to adapt their homes should ensure that older persons can \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>remain in their homes and lead autonomous lives.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>110. <b>States should establish tax incentives and subsidies to encourage developers to \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>build accessible and appropriate housing for older persons. Credit facilities should \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>also be encouraged in public and private banks to allow older persons to adapt their \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>homes or to become homeowners. In view of the difficulties often faced by older \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>persons in accessing financial and insurance services and resources, the Independent \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>Expert wishes to remind businesses of their obligations to adhere to international \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>standards preventing, inter alia, all forms of discrimination and to the Guiding \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>Principles on Business and Human Rights, which provide guidance on responsible \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>contracting and State-investor contract negotiations.</b> \u000a</p>\u000a<p>111. <b>Given that the physical ability, individual characteristics and the transport \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>environment crucially influence the mobility of older persons, States are encouraged \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>to adopt comprehensive national accessibility policies. There is also a need to include \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>mandatory provisions demanding the implementation of barrier-free access. States \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>should also ensure the elimination of existing barriers in public spaces and buildings, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>including in care settings. The Independent Expert recommends that architects and \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>engineers apply a human rights-based approach in designing public and private \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>buildings. </b></p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p>112. States should ensure older persons&#8217; mobility, including affordable and \u000a</p>\u000a<p><b>accessible public transport, both in urban, rural and remote areas. This could \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>encompass free or discounted transport, low-floor buses and trains, and facilities to \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>help older persons purchase tickets online or in person. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>  Education, training and lifelong learning \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>113. <b>The promotion of lifelong learning is essential for older persons to be able to \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>deal with constantly changing circumstances, requirements and challenges, for their \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>active participation in society and for an autonomous life continuing into old age. The \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>specific needs of older persons should be taken into account in the planning and \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>design of educational offers. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>114. <b>Distance learning and digital training should be offered to older persons in \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>order to bridge the gap among generations and avoid dependency on others as a result \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>of to the lack of knowledge of information and communications technology.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>  Care \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>115. <b>States should improve coordination among sectors throughout the continuum \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>of care, from prevention, promotion, rehabilitation, through to long-term and \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>palliative care, including social care and other community services, and prevent \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>unnecessary institutionalization. Universal coverage and uniform national legislation \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>should be introduced in order to avoid fragmentation of social and health services. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>116. <b>Older persons should be included in the design, planning, implementation and \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>evaluation of care, be these social or health-related services and facilities. Gender, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>disability and cultural-sensitivity programmes should be integrated in every care \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>setting in order to take into account the diversity of older persons and meet their \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>requirements and needs.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>117. <b>States should provide assistance to families and to other informal caregivers. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>This should include human rights, medical and human resources training, counselling, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>and financial, social and psychological support. States should strengthen mechanisms \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>to officially recognize the work undertaken by informal caregivers, including, as \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>appropriate, work permits that allow flexible schedules in order to combine paid work \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>with informal care of older persons, as well as their inclusion in the social security \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>system. Particular attention should be given to older women in their role as \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>caregivers. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>118. <b>States should develop national home-care programmes and community-based \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>care services, in rural and remote areas alike. Such programmes and services should \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>be designed and implemented in consultation with the older persons themselves and \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>their families.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>119. <b>States should provide long-term care through a comprehensive and \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>intersectoral approach, and promote the transfer of older persons from an \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>institutional to a community-care residence and home, if the older person so wishes. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>120. <b>States should develop training programmes to improve the self-care of older \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>persons. Older patient education may include health education and awareness of \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>pathologies, thereby increasing self-esteem and confidence.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>121. <b>As forced institutionalization violates the rights of older persons, States should \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>revise their legislation and regulations, in particular with regard to mental health-care \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>settings. A clear set of standards should be established on free informed consent in \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>care settings, in particular in mental health-care settings.  </b></p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p>122. <b>Residential councils that include older persons should be established in \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>institutional care settings to promote the active participation of these persons in the \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>organization of their own daily activities.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>123. <b>States should establish quality monitoring and effective and transparent \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>accountability mechanisms for public and private care settings, which take into \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>account older persons&#8217; assessments and evaluations. This requires establish<b>ing clearly \u000adefined benchmarks, such as codes of practices and conduct, and where conformity \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>can be assessed and verified by sufficient and well-trained staff both in home and \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>institutionalized care settings. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>124. <b>Quality care is also correlated with the working conditions and well-being of \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>care workers. In order to ensure that care workers provide care that meets the \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>emotional and physical needs of older persons with compassion and dignity, and in \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>order to attract people into the care sector and retain them there, better training \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>opportunities, including academic qualifications, should be provided. This, in turn, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>will also help portray care work as a profession with good career prospects. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>125. <b>Given the multidimensionality of abuse and violence against older persons, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>there is a need to adopt a comprehensive, integrated and inclusive approach involving \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>different disciplines, organizations and actors, as well as older persons themselves, in \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>identifying appropriate responses to abuse and violence. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>126. <b>States should adopt legislation and policies to prevent, detect, investigate, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>prosecute and criminalize elder abuse. Procedures must be in place for reporting \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>abuse and violence, including in public and private care settings. The Independent \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>Expert calls on States to pay particular attention to protecting victims from \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>retaliation, especially in cases where the abuse or violence is committed by a family \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>member, relative or is the result of an intimate relationship. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>127. <b>Collective prejudice against older people and general public awareness \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>influence the way in which abuse and violence is perceived, recognized and reported. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>States should devise an awareness-raising strategy regarding the issue of abuse of and \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>violence against older persons. Awareness-raising campaigns should not only target \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>older persons themselves, but also their social environment, such as family, friends \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>and caregivers. They should also target employees in homes and institutions, doctors, \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>nurses and caregivers and the broader communities.  \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>128. <b>Information about remedies, referral pathways and available services should be \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>made widely available to older persons and the broader public domain, and \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>particularly the social environment of older persons. Dissemination of information to \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>older persons about their rights could help to further improve disclosure about \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>abusive experiences and make more effective the implementation of laws related to \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>elder abuse when such laws exist. Caregivers and law enforcement personnel should \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>receive training to recognize and handle cases of abuse and violence against older \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>persons. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>129. <b>States should develop ombudsman-like positions inside institutionalized care \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>settings that could improve older persons&#8217; care and quality of life and denounce cases \u000a<b>of abuse and violence, including health-care fraud. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>130. <b>Older persons have different patterns of disease presentation from younger \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>adults, they respond to treatments and therapies in different ways and they frequently \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>have complex social needs that are related to their chronic medical conditions. States \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>should therefore ensure the availability of geriatric and gerontological specialists in \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>different types of care services and facilities. There is a need to further promote \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>geriatric medicine to ensure that a sufficient number of qualified geriatrics are \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>available to meet the needs of an ageing society. </b></p>\u000a\u000a\u000a<p>131. <b>The right to palliative care should be enshrined in the legal framework so that \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>older persons can enjoy the last years of their lives in dignity and without unnecessary \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>suffering. States should ensure the availability and accessibility of palliative care for \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>all older persons in need, particularly those who suffer from a life-threatening or life-\u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>limiting illness. Training, and adequate and affordable medication and therapeutic \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>measures, should be provided in public and private care settings. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>132. <b>Innovative care for older persons with chronic and degenerative diseases, such \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>as dementia, should be encouraged through public and private partnerships, including \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>scientific and academic sectors, in order to improve the quality of life and well-being \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p><b>of those affected. \u000a</b></p>\u000a<p>_______________<b> </b></p>\u000a\u000a</body></html>
